<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Linear algebra</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Elementary_algebra" title="Elementary algebra">Elementary algebra</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linear_subspaces_with_shading.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="236" data-file-width="325" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Linear_subspaces_with_shading.svg/250px-Linear_subspaces_with_shading.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Linear_subspaces_with_shading.svg/375px-Linear_subspaces_with_shading.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Linear_subspaces_with_shading.svg/500px-Linear_subspaces_with_shading.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linear_subspaces_with_shading.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The three-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_space" title="Euclidean space">Euclidean space</a> <b>R</b><sup>3</sup> is a vector space, and lines and planes passing through the <a href="/wiki/Origin_(mathematics)" title="Origin (mathematics)">origin</a> are vector subspaces in <b>R</b><sup>3</sup>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Linear algebra</b> is the branch of <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> concerning <a href="/wiki/Vector_space" title="Vector space">vector spaces</a> and <a href="/wiki/Linear_map" title="Linear map">linear mappings</a> between such spaces. It includes the study of lines, planes, and subspaces, but is also concerned with properties common to all vector spaces.</p>
<p>The set of points with coordinates that satisfy a linear equation forms a <a href="/wiki/Hyperplane" title="Hyperplane">hyperplane</a> in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-dimensional_space" title="N-dimensional space"><i>n</i>-dimensional space</a>. The conditions under which a set of <i>n</i> hyperplanes intersect in a single point is an important focus of study in linear algebra. Such an investigation is initially motivated by a <a href="/wiki/System_of_linear_equations" title="System of linear equations">system of linear equations</a> containing several unknowns. Such equations are naturally represented using the formalism of <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(mathematics)" title="Matrix (mathematics)">matrices</a> and vectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Linear algebra is central to both pure and applied mathematics. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Abstract_algebra" title="Abstract algebra">abstract algebra</a> arises by relaxing the axioms of a vector space, leading to a number of generalizations. <a href="/wiki/Functional_analysis" title="Functional analysis">Functional analysis</a> studies the infinite-dimensional version of the theory of vector spaces. Combined with calculus, linear algebra facilitates the solution of linear systems of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Differential_equations" title="Differential equations">differential equations</a>.</p>
<p>Techniques from linear algebra are also used in <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">analytic geometry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_animation" title="Computer animation">computer animation</a>, advanced facial recognition algorithms and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_sciences" title="Social sciences">social sciences</a> (particularly in <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>). Because linear algebra is such a well-developed theory, nonlinear <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_model" title="Mathematical model">mathematical models</a> are sometimes approximated by linear models.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The study of linear algebra first emerged from the study of <a href="/wiki/Determinant" title="Determinant">determinants</a>, which were used to solve systems of linear equations. Determinants were used by <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a> in 1693, and subsequently, <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Cramer" title="Gabriel Cramer">Gabriel Cramer</a> devised <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cramer%27s_Rule" title="Cramer's Rule">Cramer's Rule</a> for solving linear systems in 1750. Later, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gauss" title="Gauss">Gauss</a> further developed the theory of solving linear systems by using <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_elimination" title="Gaussian elimination">Gaussian elimination</a>, which was initially listed as an advancement in <a href="/wiki/Geodesy" title="Geodesy">geodesy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vitulli.2C_Marie_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vitulli.2C_Marie-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The study of matrix algebra first emerged in England in the mid-1800s. In 1844 <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Grassmann" title="Hermann Grassmann">Hermann Grassmann</a> published his "Theory of Extension" which included foundational new topics of what is today called linear algebra. In 1848, <a href="/wiki/James_Joseph_Sylvester" title="James Joseph Sylvester">James Joseph Sylvester</a> introduced the term matrix, which is Latin for "womb". While studying compositions of linear transformations, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Cayley" title="Arthur Cayley">Arthur Cayley</a> was led to define matrix multiplication and inverses. Crucially, Cayley used a single letter to denote a matrix, thus treating a matrix as an aggregate object. He also realized the connection between matrices and determinants, and wrote "There would be many things to say about this theory of matrices which should, it seems to me, precede the theory of determinants".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vitulli.2C_Marie_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vitulli.2C_Marie-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1882, <a href="/wiki/H%C3%BCseyin_Tevfik_Pasha" title="Hüseyin Tevfik Pasha">Hüseyin Tevfik Pasha</a> wrote the book titled "Linear Algebra".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The first modern and more precise definition of a vector space was introduced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peano" title="Peano">Peano</a> in 1888;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vitulli.2C_Marie_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vitulli.2C_Marie-4">[4]</a></sup> by 1900, a theory of linear transformations of finite-dimensional vector spaces had emerged. Linear algebra took its modern form in the first half of the twentieth century, when many ideas and methods of previous centuries were generalized as <a href="/wiki/Abstract_algebra" title="Abstract algebra">abstract algebra</a>. The use of matrices in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a> helped spread the subject of linear algebra beyond pure mathematics. The development of computers led to increased research in efficient <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithms</a> for Gaussian elimination and matrix decompositions, and linear algebra became an essential tool for modelling and simulations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vitulli.2C_Marie_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Vitulli.2C_Marie-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The origin of many of these ideas is discussed in the articles on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Determinants" title="Determinants">determinants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_elimination" title="Gaussian elimination">Gaussian elimination</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Educational_history">Educational history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Educational history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Linear algebra first appeared in American graduate textbooks in the 1940s and in undergraduate textbooks in the 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tucker1993_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker1993-7">[7]</a></sup> Following work by the <a href="/wiki/School_Mathematics_Study_Group" title="School Mathematics Study Group">School Mathematics Study Group</a>, U.S. high schools asked 12th grade students to do "matrix algebra, formerly reserved for college" in the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChangingCurriculum_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChangingCurriculum-8">[8]</a></sup> In France during the 1960s, educators attempted to teach linear algebra through affine dimensional vector spaces in the first year of secondary school. This was met with a backlash in the 1980s that removed linear algebra from the curriculum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In 1993, the U.S.-based Linear Algebra Curriculum Study Group recommended that undergraduate linear algebra courses be given an application-based "matrix orientation" as opposed to a theoretical orientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlgCur1993_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-AlgCur1993-10">[10]</a></sup> Reviews of the teaching of linear algebra call for stress on visualization and geometric interpretation of theoretical ideas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> and to include the <i>jewel in the crown</i> of linear algebra, the <a href="/wiki/Singular_value_decomposition" title="Singular value decomposition">singular value decomposition</a> (SVD), as 'so many other disciplines use it'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> To better suit 21st century applications, such as data mining and uncertainty analysis, linear algebra can be based upon the SVD instead of Gaussian Elimination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scope_of_study">Scope of study</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Scope of study">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vector_spaces">Vector spaces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Vector spaces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The main structures of linear algebra are <a href="/wiki/Vector_space" title="Vector space">vector spaces</a>. A vector space over a <a href="/wiki/Field_(mathematics)" title="Field (mathematics)">field</a> <i>F</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set (mathematics)">set</a> <i>V</i> together with two <a href="/wiki/Binary_operation" title="Binary operation">binary operations</a>. Elements of <i>V</i> are called <i>vectors</i> and <a href="/wiki/Element_(mathematics)" title="Element (mathematics)">elements</a> of <i>F</i> are called <i>scalars</i>. The first operation, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_addition" title="Vector addition">vector addition</a></i>, takes any two vectors <i>v</i> and <i>w</i> and outputs a third vector <span class="nowrap"><i>v</i> + <i>w</i></span>. The second operation, <i><a href="/wiki/Scalar_multiplication" title="Scalar multiplication">scalar multiplication</a></i>, takes any scalar <i>a</i> and any vector <i>v</i> and outputs a new <span class="nowrap">vector <i>av</i></span>. The operations of addition and multiplication in a vector space must satisfy the following <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axioms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In the list below, let <i>u</i>, <i>v</i> and <i>w</i> be arbitrary vectors in <i>V</i>, and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> scalars in <i>F</i>.</p>
<table border="0" style="width:100%;">
<tr>
<td><b>Axiom</b></td>
<td><b>Signification</b></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Associativity" title="Associativity">Associativity</a> of addition</td>
<td><i>u</i> + (<i>v</i> + <i>w</i>) = (<i>u</i> + <i>v</i>) + <i>w</i></td>
</tr>
<tr style="background:#F8F4FF;">
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commutativity" title="Commutativity">Commutativity</a> of addition</td>
<td><i>u</i> + <i>v</i> = <i>v</i> + <i>u</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Identity_element" title="Identity element">Identity element</a> of addition</td>
<td>There exists an element 0 ∈ <i>V</i>, called the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zero_vector" title="Zero vector">zero vector</a></i>, such that <i>v</i> + 0 = <i>v</i> for all <i>v</i> ∈ <i>V</i>.</td>
</tr>
<tr style="background:#F8F4FF;">
<td><a href="/wiki/Inverse_element" title="Inverse element">Inverse elements</a> of addition</td>
<td>For every <i>v</i> ∈ V, there exists an element −<i>v</i> ∈ <i>V</i>, called the <i><a href="/wiki/Additive_inverse" title="Additive inverse">additive inverse</a></i> of <i>v</i>, such that <i>v</i> + (−<i>v</i>) = 0</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distributivity" title="Distributivity">Distributivity</a> of scalar multiplication with respect to vector addition  </td>
<td><i>a</i>(<i>u</i> + <i>v</i>) = <i>au</i> + <i>av</i></td>
</tr>
<tr style="background:#F8F4FF;">
<td>Distributivity of scalar multiplication with respect to field addition</td>
<td>(<i>a</i> + <i>b</i>)<i>v</i> = <i>av</i> + <i>bv</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Compatibility of scalar multiplication with field multiplication</td>
<td><i>a</i>(<i>bv</i>) = (<i>ab</i>)<i>v</i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[nb 1]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr style="background:#F8F4FF;">
<td>Identity element of scalar multiplication</td>
<td>1<i>v</i> = <i>v</i>, where 1 denotes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiplicative_identity" title="Multiplicative identity">multiplicative identity</a> in <b>F</b>.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The first four axioms are those of <i>V</i> being an <a href="/wiki/Abelian_group" title="Abelian group">abelian group</a> under vector addition. Vector spaces may be diverse in nature, for example, containing <a href="/wiki/Function_(mathematics)" title="Function (mathematics)">functions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polynomial_ring" title="Polynomial ring">polynomials</a> or matrices. Linear algebra is concerned with properties common to all vector spaces.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linear_transformations">Linear transformations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Linear transformations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Linear_map" title="Linear map">Linear map</a></div>
<p>Similarly as in the theory of other algebraic structures, linear algebra studies mappings between vector spaces that preserve the vector-space structure. Given two vector spaces <i>V</i> and <i>W</i> over a field <b>F</b>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_transformation" title="Linear transformation">linear transformation</a> (also called linear map, linear mapping or linear operator) is a <a href="/wiki/Map_(mathematics)" title="Map (mathematics)">map</a></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>T</mi>
<mo>:</mo>
<mi>V</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
<mi>W</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle T:V\to W}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="T:V\to W" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4c59f606d4e06de82ae3016ab89884480356b3d6" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:11.462ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>that is compatible with addition and scalar multiplication:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mspace width="1em"></mspace>

</dl>
<p>for any vectors <i>u</i>,<i>v</i> ∈ <i>V</i> and a scalar <i>a</i> ∈ <b>F</b>.</p>
<p>Additionally for any vectors <i>u</i>, <i>v</i> ∈ <i>V</i> and scalars <i>a</i>, <i>b</i> ∈ <b>F</b>:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mo>+</mo>
<mi>b</mi>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>T</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>a</mi>
<mi>u</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>T</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>b</mi>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>a</mi>
<mi>T</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>u</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>b</mi>
<mi>T</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \quad T(au+bv)=T(au)+T(bv)=aT(u)+bT(v)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\quad T(au+bv)=T(au)+T(bv)=aT(u)+bT(v)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5e2a21c89a2d38b73f9b2ad2a7136e40b506afc1" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:48.657ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>When a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bijective" title="Bijective">bijective</a> linear mapping exists between two vector spaces (that is, every vector from the second space is associated with exactly one in the first), we say that the two spaces are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isomorphic" title="Isomorphic">isomorphic</a>. Because an isomorphism preserves linear structure, two isomorphic vector spaces are "essentially the same" from the linear algebra point of view. One essential question in linear algebra is whether a mapping is an isomorphism or not, and this question can be answered by checking if the <a href="/wiki/Determinant" title="Determinant">determinant</a> is nonzero. If a mapping is not an isomorphism, linear algebra is interested in finding its <a href="/wiki/Range_(mathematics)" title="Range (mathematics)">range</a> (or image) and the set of elements that get mapped to zero, called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kernel_(linear_operator)" title="Kernel (linear operator)">kernel</a> of the mapping.</p>
<p>Linear transformations have geometric significance. For example, <a href="/wiki/2_%C3%97_2_real_matrices" title="2 × 2 real matrices">2 × 2 real matrices</a> denote standard planar mappings that preserve the <a href="/wiki/Origin_(mathematics)" title="Origin (mathematics)">origin</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subspaces.2C_span.2C_and_basis">Subspaces, span, and basis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Subspaces, span, and basis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Linear_subspace" title="Linear subspace">Linear subspace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linear_span" title="Linear span">Linear span</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Basis_(linear_algebra)" title="Basis (linear algebra)">Basis (linear algebra)</a></div>
<p>Again, in analogue with theories of other algebraic objects, linear algebra is interested in subsets of vector spaces that are themselves vector spaces; these subsets are called <a href="/wiki/Linear_subspace" title="Linear subspace">linear subspaces</a>. For example, both the range and kernel of a linear mapping are subspaces, and are thus often called the range space and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nullspace" title="Nullspace">nullspace</a>; these are important examples of subspaces. Another important way of forming a subspace is to take a <a href="/wiki/Linear_combination" title="Linear combination">linear combination</a> of a set of vectors <i>v</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>v</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>v<sub>k</sub></i>:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>+</mo>
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>+</mo>
<mo>⋯<!-- ⋯ --></mo>
<mo>+</mo>
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>k</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>k</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a_{1}v_{1}+a_{2}v_{2}+\cdots +a_{k}v_{k},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="a_{1}v_{1}+a_{2}v_{2}+\cdots +a_{k}v_{k}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e15d3619d42b28662c5952c9ece60cd928e31774" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:25.517ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <i>a</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>a</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>a</i><sub><i>k</i></sub> are scalars. The set of all linear combinations of vectors <i>v</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>v</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>v<sub>k</sub></i> is called their <a href="/wiki/Linear_span" title="Linear span">span</a>, which forms a subspace.</p>
<p>A linear combination of any system of vectors with all zero coefficients is the zero vector of <i>V</i>. If this is the only way to express the zero vector as a linear combination of <i>v</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>v</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>v<sub>k</sub></i> then these vectors are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linearly_independent" title="Linearly independent">linearly independent</a>. Given a set of vectors that span a space, if any vector <i>w</i> is a linear combination of other vectors (and so the set is not linearly independent), then the span would remain the same if we remove <i>w</i> from the set. Thus, a set of linearly dependent vectors is redundant in the sense that there will be a linearly independent subset which will span the same subspace. Therefore, we are mostly interested in a linearly independent set of vectors that spans a vector space <i>V</i>, which we call a <a href="/wiki/Basis_(linear_algebra)" title="Basis (linear algebra)">basis</a> of <i>V</i>. Any set of vectors that spans <i>V</i> contains a basis, and any linearly independent set of vectors in <i>V</i> can be extended to a basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> It turns out that if we accept the <a href="/wiki/Axiom_of_choice" title="Axiom of choice">axiom of choice</a>, every vector space has a basis;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> nevertheless, this basis may be unnatural, and indeed, may not even be constructible. For instance, there exists a basis for the real numbers, considered as a vector space over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rationals" title="Rationals">rationals</a>, but no explicit basis has been constructed.</p>
<p>Any two bases of a vector space <i>V</i> have the same <a href="/wiki/Cardinality" title="Cardinality">cardinality</a>, which is called the <a href="/wiki/Dimension_(vector_space)" title="Dimension (vector space)">dimension</a> of <i>V</i>. The dimension of a vector space is <a href="/wiki/Well-defined" title="Well-defined">well-defined</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Dimension_theorem_for_vector_spaces" title="Dimension theorem for vector spaces">dimension theorem for vector spaces</a>. If a basis of <i>V</i> has finite number of elements, <i>V</i> is called a finite-dimensional vector space. If <i>V</i> is finite-dimensional and <i>U</i> is a subspace of <i>V</i>, then dim <i>U</i> ≤ dim <i>V</i>. If <i>U</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>U</i><sub>2</sub> are subspaces of <i>V</i>, then</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>dim</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<msub>
<mi>U</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>+</mo>
<msub>
<mi>U</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>dim</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>U</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>dim</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>U</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>dim</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<msub>
<mi>U</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>∩<!-- ∩ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>U</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \dim(U_{1}+U_{2})=\dim U_{1}+\dim U_{2}-\dim(U_{1}\cap U_{2})}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\dim(U_{1}+U_{2})=\dim U_{1}+\dim U_{2}-\dim(U_{1}\cap U_{2})" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/eea3c7fc04241d4a050008b2ba71b2dfc0d540ac" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:50.273ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>One often restricts consideration to finite-dimensional vector spaces. A fundamental theorem of linear algebra states that all vector spaces of the same dimension are isomorphic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> giving an easy way of characterizing isomorphism.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Matrix_theory">Matrix theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Matrix theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(mathematics)" title="Matrix (mathematics)">Matrix (mathematics)</a></div>
<p>A particular basis {<i>v</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>v</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>v<sub>n</sub></i>} of <i>V</i> allows one to construct a <a href="/wiki/Coordinate_system" title="Coordinate system">coordinate system</a> in <i>V</i>: the vector with coordinates (<i>a</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>a</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>a<sub>n</sub></i>) is the <a href="/wiki/Linear_combination" title="Linear combination">linear combination</a></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>+</mo>
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>+</mo>
<mo>⋯<!-- ⋯ --></mo>
<mo>+</mo>
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>.</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a_{1}v_{1}+a_{2}v_{2}+\cdots +a_{n}v_{n}.\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="a_{1}v_{1}+a_{2}v_{2}+\cdots +a_{n}v_{n}.\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6b7c1a6c5ef50524d3ec48ab5dfa0f92699ba0c1" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:26.164ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The condition that <i>v</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>v</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>v<sub>n</sub></i> span <i>V</i> guarantees that each vector <i>v</i> can be assigned coordinates, whereas the linear independence of <i>v</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>v</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>v<sub>n</sub></i> assures that these coordinates are unique (i.e. there is only one linear combination of the basis vectors that is equal to <i>v</i>). In this way, once a basis of a vector space <i>V</i> over <b>F</b> has been chosen, <i>V</i> may be identified with the coordinate <i>n</i>-space <b>F</b><sup><i>n</i></sup>. Under this identification, addition and scalar multiplication of vectors in <i>V</i> correspond to addition and scalar multiplication of their coordinate vectors in <b>F</b><sup><i>n</i></sup>. Furthermore, if <i>V</i> and <i>W</i> are an <i>n</i>-dimensional and <i>m</i>-dimensional vector space over <b>F</b>, and a basis of <i>V</i> and a basis of <i>W</i> have been fixed, then any linear transformation <i>T</i>: <i>V</i> → <i>W</i> may be encoded by an <i>m</i> × <i>n</i> <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(mathematics)" title="Matrix (mathematics)">matrix</a> <i>A</i> with entries in the field <b>F</b>, called the matrix of <i>T</i> with respect to these bases. Two matrices that encode the same linear transformation in different bases are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Similar_(linear_algebra)" title="Similar (linear algebra)">similar</a>. Matrix theory replaces the study of linear transformations, which were defined axiomatically, by the study of matrices, which are concrete objects. This major technique distinguishes linear algebra from theories of other algebraic structures, which usually cannot be parameterized so concretely.</p>
<p>There is an important distinction between the coordinate <i>n</i>-space <b>R</b><sup><i>n</i></sup> and a general finite-dimensional vector space <i>V</i>. While <b>R</b><sup><i>n</i></sup> has a <a href="/wiki/Standard_basis" title="Standard basis">standard basis</a> {<i>e</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>e</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>e<sub>n</sub></i>}, a vector space <i>V</i> typically does not come equipped with such a basis and many different bases exist (although they all consist of the same number of elements equal to the dimension of <i>V</i>).</p>
<p>One major application of the matrix theory is calculation of <a href="/wiki/Determinant" title="Determinant">determinants</a>, a central concept in linear algebra. While determinants could be defined in a basis-free manner, they are usually introduced via a specific representation of the mapping; the value of the determinant does not depend on the specific basis. It turns out that a mapping <a href="/wiki/Invertible_matrix" title="Invertible matrix">has an inverse</a> if and only if the determinant <a href="/wiki/Inverse_element" title="Inverse element">has an inverse</a> (every non-zero real or complex number has an inverse<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>). If the determinant is zero, then the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nullspace" title="Nullspace">nullspace</a> is nontrivial. Determinants have other applications, including a systematic way of seeing if a set of vectors is linearly independent (we write the vectors as the columns of a matrix, and if the determinant of that matrix is zero, the vectors are linearly dependent). Determinants could also be used to solve systems of linear equations (see <a href="/wiki/Cramer%27s_rule" title="Cramer's rule">Cramer's rule</a>), but in real applications, <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_elimination" title="Gaussian elimination">Gaussian elimination</a> is a faster method.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eigenvalues_and_eigenvectors">Eigenvalues and eigenvectors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Eigenvalues and eigenvectors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eigenvalues_and_eigenvectors" title="Eigenvalues and eigenvectors">Eigenvalues and eigenvectors</a></div>
<p>In general, the action of a linear transformation may be quite complex. Attention to low-dimensional examples gives an indication of the variety of their types. One strategy for a general n-dimensional transformation <i>T</i> is to find "characteristic lines" that are <a href="/wiki/Invariant_(mathematics)#Invariant_set" title="Invariant (mathematics)">invariant sets</a> under <i>T</i>. If <i>v</i> is a non-zero vector such that <i>Tv</i> is a scalar multiple of <i>v</i>, then the line through 0 and <i>v</i> is an invariant set under <i>T</i> and <i>v</i> is called a <a href="/wiki/Eigenvalues_and_eigenvectors" title="Eigenvalues and eigenvectors">characteristic vector</a> or <b>eigenvector</b>. The scalar λ such that <i>Tv</i> = λ<i>v</i> is called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Characteristic_value" title="Characteristic value">characteristic value</a> or <b>eigenvalue</b> of <i>T</i>.</p>
<p>To find an eigenvector or an eigenvalue, we note that</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>T</mi>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>T</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>I</mtext>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Tv-\lambda v=(T-\lambda \,{\text{I}})v=0,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="Tv-\lambda v=(T-\lambda \,{\text{I}})v=0," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4e5666d0efd896ffeac8b42d8e347569b7c5dfbb" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:26.256ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where I is the <a href="/wiki/Identity_matrix" title="Identity matrix">identity matrix</a>. For there to be nontrivial solutions to that equation, det(<i>T</i> − λ I) = 0. The determinant is a <a href="/wiki/Polynomial" title="Polynomial">polynomial</a>, and so the eigenvalues are not guaranteed to exist if the field is <b>R</b>. Thus, we often work with an <a href="/wiki/Algebraically_closed_field" title="Algebraically closed field">algebraically closed field</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex numbers</a> when dealing with eigenvectors and eigenvalues so that an eigenvalue will always exist. It would be particularly nice if given a transformation <i>T</i> taking a vector space <i>V</i> into itself we can find a basis for <i>V</i> consisting of eigenvectors. If such a basis exists, we can easily compute the action of the transformation on any vector: if <i>v</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>v</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>v<sub>n</sub></i> are linearly independent eigenvectors of a mapping of <i>n</i>-dimensional spaces <i>T</i> with (not necessarily distinct) eigenvalues λ<sub>1</sub>, λ<sub>2</sub>, ..., λ<sub><i>n</i></sub>, and if <i>v</i> = <i>a</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i><sub>1</sub> + ... + <i>a<sub>n</sub> v<sub>n</sub></i>, then,</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<msub>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<msub>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>

<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msub>
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle T(v)=T(a_{1}v_{1})+\cdots +T(a_{n}v_{n})=a_{1}T(v_{1})+\cdots +a_{n}T(v_{n})=a_{1}\lambda _{1}v_{1}+\cdots +a_{n}\lambda _{n}v_{n}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="T(v)=T(a_{1}v_{1})+\cdots +T(a_{n}v_{n})=a_{1}T(v_{1})+\cdots +a_{n}T(v_{n})=a_{1}\lambda _{1}v_{1}+\cdots +a_{n}\lambda _{n}v_{n}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/43025df9d56f9568b24ab8e115eb4619195ea09c" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:86.827ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Such a transformation is called a <a href="/wiki/Diagonalizable_matrix" title="Diagonalizable matrix">diagonalizable matrix</a> since in the eigenbasis, the transformation is represented by a <a href="/wiki/Diagonal_matrix" title="Diagonal matrix">diagonal matrix</a>. Because operations like matrix multiplication, matrix inversion, and determinant calculation are simple on diagonal matrices, computations involving matrices are much simpler if we can bring the matrix to a diagonal form. Not all matrices are diagonalizable (even over an algebraically closed field).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inner-product_spaces">Inner-product spaces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Inner-product spaces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Inner_product_space" title="Inner product space">Inner product space</a></div>
<p>Besides these basic concepts, linear algebra also studies vector spaces with additional structure, such as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_product" title="Inner product">inner product</a>. The inner product is an example of a <a href="/wiki/Bilinear_form" title="Bilinear form">bilinear form</a>, and it gives the vector space a geometric structure by allowing for the definition of length and angles. Formally, an <i>inner product</i> is a map</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟨<!-- ⟨ --></mo>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mo>,</mo>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟩<!-- ⟩ --></mo>
<mo>:</mo>
<mi>V</mi>
<mo>×<!-- × --></mo>
<mi>V</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
<mi>F</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \langle \cdot ,\cdot \rangle :V\times V\rightarrow F}</annotation>
</semantics>

</dl>
<p>that satisfies the following three <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axioms</a> for all vectors <i>u</i>, <i>v</i>, <i>w</i> in <i>V</i> and all scalars <i>a</i> in <i>F</i>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jain_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jain-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prugovec.CC.86ki_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prugovec.CC.86ki-23">[22]</a></sup></p>

</ul>
<dl>

<p>We can define the length of a vector <i>v</i> in <i>V</i> by</p>

<dl>

<p>In particular, the quantity</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</dl>
<p>and so we can call this quantity the cosine of the angle between the two vectors.</p>
<p>Two vectors are orthogonal if <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟨<!-- ⟨ --></mo>
<mi>u</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟩<!-- ⟩ --></mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \langle u,v\rangle =0}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\langle u,v\rangle =0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/387ba1508966878a7437d870071b2057ae4a27fb" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:9.635ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>. An orthonormal basis is a basis where all basis vectors have length 1 and are orthogonal to each other. Given any finite-dimensional vector space, an orthonormal basis could be found by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gram%E2%80%93Schmidt" title="Gram–Schmidt">Gram–Schmidt</a> procedure. Orthonormal bases are particularly nice to deal with, since if <i>v</i> = <i>a</i><sub>1</sub> <i>v</i><sub>1</sub> + ... + <i>a<sub>n</sub> v<sub>n</sub></i>, then <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟨<!-- ⟨ --></mo>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟩<!-- ⟩ --></mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a_{i}=\langle v,v_{i}\rangle }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="a_{i}=\langle v,v_{i}\rangle " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c28af6afe0a2c25c8df2dae4f238bbb75a9d6e1c" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.114ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>.</p>
<p>The inner product facilitates the construction of many useful concepts. For instance, given a transform <i>T</i>, we can define its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermitian_conjugate" title="Hermitian conjugate">Hermitian conjugate</a> <i>T*</i> as the linear transform satisfying</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟨<!-- ⟨ --></mo>
<mi>T</mi>
<mi>u</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟩<!-- ⟩ --></mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟨<!-- ⟨ --></mo>
<mi>u</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<msup>
<mi>T</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟩<!-- ⟩ --></mo>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \langle Tu,v\rangle =\langle u,T^{*}v\rangle .}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\langle Tu,v\rangle =\langle u,T^{*}v\rangle ." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d7a79d34cec7daccbc00f8d5dcdbbcb113670eab" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:18.911ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>If <i>T</i> satisfies <i>TT*</i> = <i>T*T</i>, we call <i>T</i> <a href="/wiki/Normal_matrix" title="Normal matrix">normal</a>. It turns out that normal matrices are precisely the matrices that have an orthonormal system of eigenvectors that span <i>V</i>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Some_main_useful_theorems">Some main useful theorems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Some main useful theorems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>A matrix is invertible, or non-singular, if and only if the <a href="/wiki/Linear_map" title="Linear map">linear map</a> represented by the matrix is an <a href="/wiki/Isomorphism" title="Isomorphism">isomorphism</a>.</li>
<li>Any vector space over a field <b>F</b> of dimension <i>n</i> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isomorphic" title="Isomorphic">isomorphic</a> to <b>F</b><sup><i>n</i></sup> as a vector space over <b>F</b>.</li>
<li>Corollary: Any two vector spaces over <b>F</b> of the same finite dimension are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isomorphic" title="Isomorphic">isomorphic</a> to each other.</li>
<li>A linear map is an isomorphism if and only if the <a href="/wiki/Determinant" title="Determinant">determinant</a> is nonzero.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Because of the ubiquity of vector spaces, linear algebra is used in many fields of mathematics, natural sciences, computer science, and social science. Below are just some examples of applications of linear algebra.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solution_of_linear_systems">Solution of linear systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Solution of linear systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/System_of_linear_equations" title="System of linear equations">System of linear equations</a></div>
<p>Linear algebra provides the formal setting for the linear combination of equations used in the Gaussian method. Suppose the goal is to find and describe the solution(s), if any, of the following system of linear equations:</p>

<p>The Gaussian-elimination algorithm is as follows: eliminate <i>x</i> from all equations below <i>L</i><sub>1</sub>, and then eliminate <i>y</i> from all equations below <i>L</i><sub>2</sub>. This will put the system into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triangular_form" title="Triangular form">triangular form</a>. Then, using back-substitution, each unknown can be solved for.</p>
<p>In the example, <i>x</i> is eliminated from <i>L</i><sub>2</sub> by adding (3/2)<i>L</i><sub>1</sub> to <i>L</i><sub>2</sub>. <i>x</i> is then eliminated from <i>L</i><sub>3</sub> by adding <i>L</i><sub>1</sub> to <i>L</i><sub>3</sub>. Formally:</p>

<p>This result is a system of linear equations in triangular form, and so the first part of the algorithm is complete.</p>
<p>The last part, back-substitution, consists of solving for the known in reverse order. It can thus be seen that</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle z=-1\quad (L_{3})}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="z=-1\quad (L_{3})" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9f8481344e499b938f933118d2ea2e722ca19169" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:14.007ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Then, <i>z</i> can be substituted into <i>L</i><sub>2</sub>, which can then be solved to obtain</p>
<dl>

<dl>

</mrow>

</dl>
<p>The system is solved.</p>
<p>We can, in general, write any system of linear equations as a matrix equation:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>A</mi>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>b</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Ax=b.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="Ax=b." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/88450cdee78093e5a035c0c478a4749535b46f72" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:7.868ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The solution of this system is characterized as follows: first, we find a particular solution <i>x</i><sub>0</sub> of this equation using Gaussian elimination. Then, we compute the solutions of <i>Ax</i> = 0; that is, we find the null space <i>N</i> of <i>A</i>. The solution set of this equation is given by <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>N</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">{</mo>
<msub>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo>:</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo>∈<!-- ∈ --></mo>
<mi>N</mi>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">}</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x_{0}+N=\{x_{0}+n:n\in N\}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x_{0}+N=\{x_{0}+n:n\in N\}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/69efe4b601559a9e14bf8f6b025fd6b4bbaef515" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:27.717ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>. If the number of variables is equal to the number of equations, then we can characterize when the system has a unique solution: since <i>N</i> is trivial if and only if det <i>A</i> ≠ 0, the equation has a unique solution if and only if det <i>A</i> ≠ 0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linear_Algebra_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Linear_Algebra-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Least-squares_best_fit_line">Least-squares best fit line</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Least-squares best fit line">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Linear_least_squares_(mathematics)" title="Linear least squares (mathematics)">least squares method</a> is used to determine the best fit line for a set of data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Least_Squares_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Least_Squares-25">[24]</a></sup> This line will minimize the sum of the squares of the residuals.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fourier_series_expansion">Fourier series expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Fourier series expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fourier_series" title="Fourier series">Fourier series</a> are a representation of a function <i>f</i>: [−π, π] → <b>R</b> as a trigonometric series:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>f</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<munderover>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">∞<!-- ∞ --></mi>
</mrow>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f(x)={\frac {a_{0}}{2}}+\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }\,[a_{n}\cos(nx)+b_{n}\sin(nx)].}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f(x)={\frac {a_{0}}{2}}+\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }\,[a_{n}\cos(nx)+b_{n}\sin(nx)]." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7bfd233836a00d8972d09c8ed15fd5f147e0cb64" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:42.787ex; height:7.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>This series expansion is extremely useful in solving <a href="/wiki/Partial_differential_equation" title="Partial differential equation">partial differential equations</a>. In this article, we will not be concerned with convergence issues; it is nice to note that all Lipschitz-continuous functions have a converging Fourier series expansion, and <a href="/wiki/Dirichlet_conditions" title="Dirichlet conditions">nice enough</a> discontinuous functions have a Fourier series that converges to the function value at most points.</p>
<p>The space of all functions that can be represented by a Fourier series form a vector space (technically speaking, we call functions that have the same Fourier series expansion the "same" function, since two different discontinuous functions might have the same Fourier series). Moreover, this space is also an <a href="/wiki/Inner_product_space" title="Inner product space">inner product space</a> with the inner product</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟨<!-- ⟨ --></mo>
<mi>f</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>g</mi>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">⟩<!-- ⟩ --></mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<msubsup>
<mo>∫<!-- ∫ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
</mrow>
</msubsup>
<mi>f</mi>

<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>

<mo>.</mo>

</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \langle f,g\rangle ={\frac {1}{\pi }}\int _{-\pi }^{\pi }f(x)g(x)\,dx.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\langle f,g\rangle ={\frac {1}{\pi }}\int _{-\pi }^{\pi }f(x)g(x)\,dx." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/aa6e0d4ab39ff9f83e6d45038f084a6cd6a731f1" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:27.489ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The functions <i>g<sub>n</sub></i>(<i>x</i>) = sin(<i>nx</i>) for <i>n</i> &gt; 0 and <i>h<sub>n</sub></i>(<i>x</i>) = cos(<i>nx</i>) for <i>n</i> ≥ 0 are an orthonormal basis for the space of Fourier-expandable functions. We can thus use the tools of linear algebra to find the expansion of any function in this space in terms of these basis functions. For instance, to find the coefficient <i>a<sub>k</sub></i>, we take the inner product with <i>h<sub>k</sub></i>:</p>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a_{k}={\frac {1}{\pi }}\int _{-\pi }^{\pi }f(x)\cos(kx)\,dx.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="a_{k}={\frac {1}{\pi }}\int _{-\pi }^{\pi }f(x)\cos(kx)\,dx." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4f26b17ef1ff9b7be39eefdc7aebfe82d7dfa192" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:28.16ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_mechanics">Quantum mechanics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Quantum mechanics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Quantum mechanics is highly inspired by notions in linear algebra. In <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, the physical state of a particle is represented by a vector, and observables (such as <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a>) are represented by linear operators on the underlying vector space. More concretely, the <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">wave function</a> of a particle describes its physical state and lies in the vector space <a href="/wiki/Lp_space" title="Lp space">L<sup>2</sup></a> (the functions φ: <b>R</b><sup>3</sup> → <b>C</b> such that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msubsup>
<mo>∫<!-- ∫ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∞<!-- ∞ --></mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">∞<!-- ∞ --></mi>
</mrow>
</msubsup>
<msubsup>
<mo>∫<!-- ∫ --></mo>

</mrow>

</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }|\phi |^{2}dxdydz" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a4ff42570ce7317b6a989ef5dd9dc3b99b8ce2e3" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:25.632ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span> is finite), and it evolves according to the <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schrödinger equation">Schrödinger equation</a>. Energy is represented as the operator <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</mrow>

<mo>+</mo>
<mi>V</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>y</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>z</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle H=-{\frac {\hbar ^{2}}{2m}}\nabla ^{2}+V(x,y,z)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="H=-{\frac {\hbar ^{2}}{2m}}\nabla ^{2}+V(x,y,z)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4d48b64a9ce8ce9e7c5e46731eddcb08b384b655" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:26.242ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span>, where <i>V</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a>. <i>H</i> is also known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamiltonian_operator" title="Hamiltonian operator">Hamiltonian operator</a>. The eigenvalues of <i>H</i> represents the possible energies that can be observed. Given a particle in some state φ, we can expand φ into a linear combination of eigenstates of <i>H</i>. The component of <i>H</i> in each eigenstate determines the probability of measuring the corresponding eigenvalue, and the measurement forces the particle to assume that eigenstate (wave function collapse).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometric_introduction">Geometric introduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Geometric introduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many of the principles and techniques of linear algebra can be seen in the geometry of lines in a real two dimensional plane <i>E</i>. When formulated using vectors and matrices the geometry of points and lines in the plane can be extended to the geometry of points and hyperplanes in high-dimensional spaces.</p>
<p>Point coordinates in the plane <i>E</i> are ordered pairs of real numbers, (<i>x</i>,<i>y</i>), and a line is defined as the set of points (<i>x</i>,<i>y</i>) that satisfy the <a href="/wiki/Linear_equation" title="Linear equation">linear equation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strang_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strang-26">[25]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mo>:</mo>
<mi>a</mi>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>b</mi>
<mi>y</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>c</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lambda :ax+by+c=0,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\lambda :ax+by+c=0," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f88e83a6edb82f20f47b46e7659710ca508e0014" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:19.726ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <i>a</i>, <i>b</i> and <i>c</i> are not all zero. Then,</p>
<dl>

</dl>
<p>or</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</dl>
<p>where <b>x</b> = (<i>x</i>, <i>y</i>, 1) is the 3 × 1 set of <a href="/wiki/Homogeneous_coordinates" title="Homogeneous coordinates">homogeneous coordinates</a> associated with the point (<i>x</i>, <i>y</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Semple_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Semple-27">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Homogeneous coordinates identify the plane <i>E</i> with the <i>z</i> = 1 plane in three dimensional space. The x−y coordinates in <i>E</i> are obtained from homogeneous coordinates <b>y</b> = (<i>y</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>y</i><sub>2</sub>, <i>y</i><sub>3</sub>) by dividing by the third component (if it is nonzero) to obtain <b>y</b> = (<i>y</i><sub>1</sub>/<i>y</i><sub>3</sub>, <i>y</i><sub>2</sub>/<i>y</i><sub>3</sub>, 1).</p>
<p>The linear equation, λ, has the important property, that if <b>x</b><sub>1</sub> and <b>x</b><sub>2</sub> are homogeneous coordinates of points on the line, then the point <i>α</i><b>x</b><sub>1</sub> + <i>β</i><b>x</b><sub>2</sub> is also on the line, for any real <i>α</i> and <i>β</i>.</p>
<p>Now consider the equations of the two lines <i>λ</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>λ</i><sub>2</sub>,</p>

<p>if the rows of <b>B</b> are linearly independent (i.e., <i>λ</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>λ</i><sub>2</sub> represent distinct lines). Divide through by x<sub>3</sub> to get <a href="/wiki/Cramer%27s_rule" title="Cramer's rule">Cramer's rule</a> for the solution of a set of two linear equations in two unknowns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nering_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nering-28">[27]</a></sup> Notice that this yields a point in the <i>z</i> = 1 plane only when the 2 × 2 submatrix associated with <i>x</i><sub>3</sub> has a non-zero determinant.</p>
<p>It is interesting to consider the case of three lines, λ<sub>1</sub>, λ<sub>2</sub> and λ<sub>3</sub>, which yield the matrix equation,</p>

<p>which in homogeneous form yields,</p>

</dl>
<p>Clearly, this equation has the solution <b>x</b> = (0,0,0), which is not a point on the <i>z</i> = 1 plane <i>E</i>. For a solution to exist in the plane <i>E</i>, the coefficient matrix <i>C</i> must have rank 2, which means its determinant must be zero. Another way to say this is that the columns of the matrix must be linearly dependent.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction_to_linear_transformations">Introduction to linear transformations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Introduction to linear transformations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Another way to approach linear algebra is to consider linear functions on the two dimensional real plane <i>E</i>=<b>R</b><sup>2</sup>. Here <b>R</b> denotes the set of real numbers. Let <b>x</b>=(x, y) be an arbitrary vector in <i>E</i> and consider the linear function λ: <i>E</i>→<b>R</b>, given by</p>
<dl>

<p>or</p>
<dl>

<p>This transformation has the important property that if A<b>y</b>=d, then</p>

</mrow>

</math></span><img alt="A(\alpha \mathbf {x} +\beta \mathbf {y} )=\alpha A\mathbf {x} +\beta A\mathbf {y} =\alpha c+\beta d." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e79cc1503c2672a4094e3a73b672b7448257b4c" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:38.969ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>This shows that the sum of vectors in <i>E</i> map to the sum of their images in <b>R</b>. This is the defining characteristic of a <a href="/wiki/Linear_map" title="Linear map">linear map</a>, or linear transformation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strang_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Strang-26">[25]</a></sup> For this case, where the image space is a real number the map is called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_functional" title="Linear functional">linear functional</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nering_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nering-28">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Consider the linear functional a little more carefully. Let <b>i</b>=(1,0) and <b>j</b> =(0,1) be the natural basis vectors on <i>E</i>, so that <b>x</b>=x<b>i</b>+y<b>j</b>. It is now possible to see that</p>

<p>Thus, the columns of the matrix A are the image of the basis vectors of <i>E</i> in <b>R</b>.</p>
<p>This is true for any pair of vectors used to define coordinates in <i>E</i>. Suppose we select a non-orthogonal non-unit vector basis <b>v</b> and <b>w</b> to define coordinates of vectors in <i>E</i>. <p>where A<b>v</b>=d and A<b>w</b>=e are the images of the basis vectors <b>v</b> and <b>w</b>. This is written in matrix form as</p>

<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mtd>
<mtd>
<mi>b</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
</mtable>
<mo>]</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
</msub>
</mtd>
<mtd>
<msub>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordinates_relative_to_a_basis">Coordinates relative to a basis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Coordinates relative to a basis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This leads to the question of how to determine the coordinates of a vector <b>x</b> relative to a general basis <b>v</b> and <b>w</b> in <i>E</i>. Assume that we know the coordinates of the vectors, <b>x</b>, <b>v</b> and <b>w</b> in the natural basis <b>i</b>=(1,0) and <b>j</b> =(0,1). Our goal is two find the real numbers α, β, so that <b>x</b>=α<b>v</b>+β<b>w</b>, that is</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mrow>

<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mi>x</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mi>y</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
</mtable>
<mo>}</mo>
</mrow>

<mn>1</mn>

<p>To solve this equation for α, β, we compute the linear coordinate functionals σ and τ for the basis <b>v</b>, <b>w</b>, which are given by,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Semple_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Semple-27">[26]</a></sup></p>

</dl>
<p>Thus, the matrix formed by the coordinate linear functionals is the inverse of the matrix formed by the basis vectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strang_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-Strang-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nering_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nering-28">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inverse_image">Inverse image</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Inverse image">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The set of points in the plane <i>E</i> that map to the same image in <b>R</b> under the linear functional λ define a line in <i>E</i>. This line is the image of the inverse map, λ<sup>−1</sup>: <b>R</b>→<i>E</i>. This inverse image is the set of the points <b>x</b>=(x, y) that solve the equation,</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>A</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">x</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow>
<mo>[</mo>
<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mi>a</mi>
</mtd>
<mtd>
<mi>b</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
</mtable>
<mo>]</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow>
<mo>{</mo>
<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mi>x</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mi>y</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
</mtable>
<mo>}</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>c</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle A\mathbf {x} ={\begin{bmatrix}a&amp;b\end{bmatrix}}{\begin{Bmatrix}x\\y\end{Bmatrix}}=c.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="A\mathbf {x} ={\begin{bmatrix}a&amp;b\end{bmatrix}}{\begin{Bmatrix}x\\y\end{Bmatrix}}=c." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bf2b8e456aefd8889bcb98c03e98b5d1f75fdcda" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:23.303ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Notice that a linear functional operates on known values for <b>x</b>=(x, y) to compute a value <i>c</i> in <b>R</b>, while the inverse image seeks the values for <b>x</b>=(x, y) that yield a specific value <i>c</i>.</p>
<p>In order to solve the equation, we first recognize that only one of the two unknowns (x,y) can be determined, so we select y to be determined, and rearrange the equation</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>b</mi>
<mi>y</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>c</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>a</mi>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle by=c-ax.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="by=c-ax." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c79a0ea3978eda0a48120df1a3905f56d98d6980" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:12.389ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Solve for y and obtain the inverse image as the set of points,</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">x</mi>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow>
<mo>{</mo>
<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mn>0</mn>
</mtd>
</mtr>
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mi>c</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>b</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
</mtable>
<mo>}</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow>
<mo>{</mo>
<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mn>1</mn>
</mtd>
</mtr>
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
<mi>b</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
</mtable>
<mo>}</mo>
</mrow>

<mo>=</mo>

</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">h</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbf {x} (t)={\begin{Bmatrix}0\\c/b\end{Bmatrix}}+t{\begin{Bmatrix}1\\-a/b\end{Bmatrix}}=\mathbf {p} +t\mathbf {h} .}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbf {x} (t)={\begin{Bmatrix}0\\c/b\end{Bmatrix}}+t{\begin{Bmatrix}1\\-a/b\end{Bmatrix}}=\mathbf {p} +t\mathbf {h} ." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ea836ac8abf48419c22693252496c5bda715e7f6" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:38.325ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>For convenience the free parameter x has been relabeled t.</p>
<p>The vector <b>p</b> defines the intersection of the line with the y-axis, known as the y-intercept. The vector <b>h</b> satisfies the homogeneous equation,</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">h</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow>
<mo>[</mo>
<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">

</mrow>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow>
<mo>{</mo>
<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mn>1</mn>
</mtd>
</mtr>
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
