</mrow>
<mi>b</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
</mtable>
<mo>}</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0.</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle A\mathbf {h} ={\begin{bmatrix}a&amp;b\end{bmatrix}}{\begin{Bmatrix}1\\-a/b\end{Bmatrix}}=0.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="A\mathbf {h} ={\begin{bmatrix}a&amp;b\end{bmatrix}}{\begin{Bmatrix}1\\-a/b\end{Bmatrix}}=0." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/aa43d7689f6192cb8f5caf6cc9e9fa7d0b0a2195" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:27.433ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Notice that if <b>h</b> is a solution to this homogeneous equation, then <i>t</i> <b>h</b> is also a solution.</p>
<p>The set of points of a linear functional that map to zero define the <i>kernel</i> of the linear functional. The line can be considered to be the set of points <b>h</b> in the kernel translated by the vector <b>p</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strang_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-Strang-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nering_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nering-28">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Generalizations_and_related_topics">Generalizations and related topics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Generalizations and related topics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Since linear algebra is a successful theory, its methods have been developed and generalized in other parts of mathematics. In <a href="/wiki/Module_(mathematics)" title="Module (mathematics)">module</a> theory, one replaces the <a href="/wiki/Field_(mathematics)" title="Field (mathematics)">field</a> of scalars by a ring. The concepts of linear independence, span, basis, and dimension (which is called rank in module theory) still make sense. Nevertheless, many theorems from linear algebra become false in module theory. For instance, not all modules have a basis (those that do are called <a href="/wiki/Free_module" title="Free module">free modules</a>), the rank of a free module is not necessarily unique, not every linearly independent subset of a module can be extended to form a basis, and not every subset of a module that spans the space contains a basis.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Multilinear_algebra" title="Multilinear algebra">multilinear algebra</a>, one considers multivariable linear transformations, that is, mappings that are linear in each of a number of different variables. This line of inquiry naturally leads to the idea of the <a href="/wiki/Dual_space" title="Dual space">dual space</a>, the vector space <i>V</i><sup>∗</sup> consisting of linear maps <span class="nowrap"><i>f</i>: <i>V</i> → <i>F</i></span> where <i>F</i> is the field of scalars. Multilinear maps <span class="nowrap"><i>T</i>: <i>V<sup>n</sup></i> → <i>F</i></span> can be described via <a href="/wiki/Tensor_product" title="Tensor product">tensor products</a> of elements of <i>V</i><sup>∗</sup>.</p>
<p>If, in addition to vector addition and scalar multiplication, there is a bilinear vector product <span class="nowrap"><i>V</i> × <i>V</i> → <i>V</i></span>, the vector space is called an <a href="/wiki/Algebra_over_a_field" title="Algebra over a field">algebra</a>; for instance, associative algebras are algebras with an associate vector product (like the algebra of square matrices, or the algebra of polynomials).</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Functional_analysis" title="Functional analysis">Functional analysis</a> mixes the methods of linear algebra with those of <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_analysis" title="Mathematical analysis">mathematical analysis</a> and studies various function spaces, such as <a href="/wiki/Lp_space" title="Lp space">L<sup><i>p</i></sup> spaces</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Representation_theory" title="Representation theory">Representation theory</a> studies the actions of algebraic objects on vector spaces by representing these objects as matrices. It is interested in all the ways that this is possible, and it does so by finding subspaces invariant under all transformations of the algebra. The concept of eigenvalues and eigenvectors is especially important.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Algebraic_geometry" title="Algebraic geometry">Algebraic geometry</a> considers the solutions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systems_of_polynomial_equations" title="Systems of polynomial equations">systems of polynomial equations</a>.</p>
<p>There are several related topics in the field of Computer Programming that utilizes much of the techniques and theorems Linear Algebra encompasses and refers to.</p>

<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The existence of a basis is straightforward for <a href="/wiki/Finitely_generated_module" title="Finitely generated module">countably generated</a> vector spaces, and for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Well-ordered" title="Well-ordered">well-ordered</a> vector spaces, but in <a href="/wiki/Dimension_theorem_for_vector_spaces" title="Dimension theorem for vector spaces">full generality</a> it is <a href="/wiki/Logical_equivalence" title="Logical equivalence">logically equivalent</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Axiom_of_choice" title="Axiom of choice">axiom of choice</a>.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">If we restrict to integers, then only 1 and -1 have an inverse. <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_2">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Fearnley-Sander, Desmond, "Hermann Grassmann and the Creation of Linear Algebra", American Mathematical Monthly <b>86</b> (1979), pp. 809–817.</li>
<li>Grassmann, Hermann, <i>Die lineale Ausdehnungslehre ein neuer Zweig der Mathematik: dargestellt und durch Anwendungen auf die übrigen Zweige der Mathematik, wie auch auf die Statik, Mechanik, die Lehre vom Magnetismus und die Krystallonomie erläutert</i>, O. Wigand, Leipzig, 1844.</li>
</ul>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Online_books">Online books</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linear_algebra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Online books">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Beezer, Rob, <i><a class="external text" href="http://linear.ups.edu/index.html" rel="nofollow">A First Course in Linear Algebra</a></i></li>
<li>Connell, Edwin H., <i><a class="external text" href="http://www.math.miami.edu/~ec/book/" rel="nofollow">Elements of Abstract and Linear Algebra</a></i></li>
<li>Hefferon, Jim, <i><a class="external text" href="http://joshua.smcvt.edu/linearalgebra/" rel="nofollow">Linear Algebra</a></i></li>
<li>Matthews, Keith, <i><a class="external text" href="http://www.numbertheory.org/book/" rel="nofollow">Elementary Linear Algebra</a></i></li>
<li>Sharipov, Ruslan, <i><a class="external text" href="http://arxiv.org/abs/math.HO/0405323" rel="nofollow">Course of linear algebra and multidimensional geometry</a></i></li>
<li>Treil, Sergei, <i><a class="external text" href="http://www.math.brown.edu/~treil/papers/LADW/LADW.html" rel="nofollow">Linear Algebra Done Wrong</a></i></li>
</ul>
